<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_9cc04195-fe3d-4248-a445-08c91af19c55'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer6544343916556687182">"19 avril 1936. Bientôt minuit. Je vais naître dans une minute exactement. Je vais voir le jour le 20 avril. Date anniversaire de notre Fürher. Je serai ainsi béni des dieux germaniques et l'on verra en moi le premier-né de la race suprême. La race aryenne. Celle qui désormais régnera en maître sur le monde. Je suis l'enfant du futur.   <span id="freeText6544343916556687182" style="display:none">"19 avril 1936. Bientôt minuit. Je vais naître dans une minute exactement. Je vais voir le jour le 20 avril. Date anniversaire de notre Fürher. Je serai ainsi béni des dieux germaniques et l'on verra en moi le premier-né de la race suprême. La race aryenne. Celle qui désormais régnera en maître sur le monde. Je suis l'enfant du futur. Conçu sans amour. Sans Dieu. Sans loi. Sans rien d'autre que la force et la rage. Je mordrai au lieu de téter. Je hurlerai au lieu de gazouiller. Je haïrai au lieu d'aimer. Heil Hitler!"<br /><br />Max est le prototype parfait du programme "Lebensborn" initié par Himmler. Des femmes sélectionnées par les nazis mettent au monde de purs représentants de la race aryenne, jeunesse idéale destinée à régénérer l'Allemagne puis l'Europe occupée par le Reich.<br />Une fable historique fascinante et dérangeante qu'on ne peut pas lâcher. Une lecture choc, remarquablement documentée, dont on ne sort pas indemne</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Procreation of the perfect species was all that mattered. The feelings of the men and women didn't matter, in fact early in the book Max states that i<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Procreation of the perfect species was all that mattered. The feelings of the men and women didn't matter, in fact early in the book Max states that is was important that they didn't get a taste for it. So it was a case of making the babies with the best blood and that was all that mattered.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1877475303" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1877475303" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/202724-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601676054p2/202724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1877475303?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/202724-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781626720718" href="/book/show/25689006-max">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=youngadult">youngadult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=library">library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1877475303" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1037232153722693847">This is a story that needs to be told. The Lebensborn program, where the Nazis literally bred people with superior Aryan characteristics in order to create a master race, is not something that you hear a lot about. At least, not in fiction, and certainly not in fiction presented to teens. So when I heard about this book, a book narrated by a Lebensborn beginning at his birth, I thought, Finally. This will be a striking, important book! What would it be like to be raised, from birth, on a diet of</span>
  <span id="freeText1037232153722693847" style="display:none">This is a story that needs to be told. The Lebensborn program, where the Nazis literally bred people with superior Aryan characteristics in order to create a master race, is not something that you hear a lot about. At least, not in fiction, and certainly not in fiction presented to teens. So when I heard about this book, a book narrated by a Lebensborn beginning at his birth, I thought, Finally. This will be a striking, important book! What would it be like to be raised, from birth, on a diet of Nazi propaganda? And not just that Germans are a master race, but that you, specifically, have been born a superior being? The Lebensborn did not know their fathers. They were taken from their mothers and adopted out to high-ranking Nazi officers as a reward. I have never come across a story about what happened to any of them, and my degree is basically in German literature, I am married to a man with a degree in German, and we met when we were both in a bizarre post-WWII German play. <br /><br />Needless to say, as soon as I heard about it, I had to read this book. <br /><br />And now I am angry. Not that such a thing happened, not the sort of anger that reading about WWII, or the Holocaust, causes. Not that righteous anger. No, I'm angry because the first book I've ever found about the Lebensborn program is a FREAKING HOT MESS and I want to know how anyone thought this would be okay.<br /><br />I suspect part of the problem is that it is translated from French. A lot of my issues stem from what I shall call word choice in this book. Let's give the benefit of the doubt to Ms. Cohen-Scali and say that she had a very clumsy translator. Much of the slang and several of the idioms are jarringly modern, pulling me out of the story repeatedly to frown and think, would they have known what that was in 1936? In war-torn 1941 Berlin? <br /><br />Annnnd then there's the swearing. So many swears! Now, bear with me. I'm no shrinking flower when it comes to swearing in literature (just ask my easily shocked book club.) And yes, I know that the F-word was not unknown in the 1940's. I'd just like to know how a four-year-old, raised in an environment where pure thoughts were held to such a high standard, would hear that word enough to use it constantly. Also, calling all women bitches. Let's think about this. He knows, even before he is born, that he is special. His mother is one of the finest examples of Aryan womanhood. All the women there are. They have been carefully examined and vetted, their pedigrees checked and rechecked, they are revered as goddesses doing their sacred duty. And running the Lebensborn home are a bevy of nunlike women, all of whom nearly faint at the sound of Hitler's voice on the radio. They speak constantly of higher thoughts, of taking care of beautiful Aryan bodies for the purity of the homeland, nobility of purpose for men and women, etc. etc. How then is this child then born thinking (and later speaking) not only a constant stream of venomous f-words, but also referring to all women as bitches and whores? Where did he learn this? Not one other person in the book is depicted as talking this way, so who is he modeling his speech after?<br /><br />And that's really where I have a huge problem with this book. Not the swearing, not the fact that the main character is a little jerk (which I expected), but the fact that he is so inconsistent. Inconsistent to the time period, to his upbringing, and inconsistent with his own past behavior. He will say that he forgets his real mother, or this person, or that event. Then later he will describe the person 0r the event in detail for someone, with no explanation of whether he was lying about forgetting, or something made the memory return. He has seen soldiers having sex with both prostitutes and prisoners at one house where he lives, and talks about how boring it is and how uninterested he is in girls' bodies, then a chapter later he naughtily sneaks off to spy on some naked girls. (He is also way too young at this time to know or care about what he is seeing.) (And actually that's the point where I nearly had to DNF this book. I don't know about you, but reading page after page of excruciating details about young girls being kidnaped, beaten, and then given a highly invasive gynecological exam is about my limit for gratuitous anything in a book. Ditto the descriptions of young boys' penises which seemed entirely exploitative.)<br /><br />There is a large chunk of the book where he is four years old, which made me wonder if the author had ever met a four year old. Not only does he speak and think in long, eloquent sentences, but his physical behavior is well above and beyond even an eight-year-old. He's supposed to be smart and physically "perfect" but not preternaturally smart and strong. At this time he also knows exactly what happens at concentration camps, and works for a time with a collaborator. She defies her handlers, and he is confused when he doesn't see her again and thinks perhaps she has been sent back to the camp. Years later he thinks about exactly how she was executed, as though he knew. Also, having talked since the day he was born about inferior people being killed, having spent years at a special school training to be a good Nazi, and having learned that he is using soap made from the fat of murdered Jews, when someone tells him that the Jews in concentration camps are being killed, he freaks out like he's never heard such a horrible thing. Okay, seriously?<br /><br />There's no moment of clarity where he discovers that all he knows is wrong, nor is there a moment when he decides that, no matter the evidence, the Nazi way is always right. There's no consistency to what he does and doesn't know, or how he will react. He seems to have few emotions at all, unless being arrogant counts as an emotion. Even when no praise is given, he believes himself to be superior, and it's all he thinks about. Yet he never seems to come "down" because of his hubris. The book is just a recitation of what happened in the war, as told by an arrogant little brat who keeps contradicting himself. For contrast, the one person he actually likes and looks up to is a fine Aryan specimen who is secretly Jewish. He is based on the very real Solomon Perel, a young Jewish man who attended an elite Nazi school as the ultimate cover story. (His story is told in "Europa, Europa," which is both a book and a superb movie.) This story would have been equally interesting, except he is also a jerk who does things with no rhyme or reason, therefore he is impossible to understand or root for. <br /><br />Can you see why I'm angry? The premise of this book is gold. Her research seems to have been impeccable. There's a bibliography and notes at the end. It has all the earmarks of an important book for the WWII fiction shelves. And then she drops the ball so hard it's painful to read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1037232153722693847" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10166492487768961234">If you like your books to be challenging and thought provoking then Max is your kind of novel! Credit must go to the author Sarah Cohen-Scali and translator (Text’s own Penny Hueston!) for crafting a character that despite the evil he embodies was able to invoke empathy in us as readers. We couldn’t help but feel for Max as he tries desperately to navigate a world of bleak political control and indoctrination—the consequences of which are often devastating.<br/><br/>From the beginning of the book we were </span>
  <span id="freeText10166492487768961234" style="display:none">If you like your books to be challenging and thought provoking then Max is your kind of novel! Credit must go to the author Sarah Cohen-Scali and translator (Text’s own Penny Hueston!) for crafting a character that despite the evil he embodies was able to invoke empathy in us as readers. We couldn’t help but feel for Max as he tries desperately to navigate a world of bleak political control and indoctrination—the consequences of which are often devastating.<br /><br />From the beginning of the book we were immersed in the world through Max’s eyes and it was this gripping perspective that kept us reading. The haunting suspense that came—not just from the persistent propaganda that surrounds Max—but from the unlikely friendship that develops between Max and a Polish boy Lukas, is what makes this book an undeniable page-turner.<br /><br />As one of our Text team members, Rights Coordinator, Alice Cottrell said:<br /><br />'People tend to baulk when I explain that the first part of the story is narrated by a foetus—the shining star of the Nazi eugenics program—but Max is a fascinating and sensitive exploration of the power of propaganda and indoctrination.'<br /><br />Bold, unsettling and confronting, it’s not hard to see why this daring work of fiction has won twelve French literary prizes.<br /><br />You can read a preview of the book here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.textpublishing.com.au/previews/max">www.textpublishing.com.au/previews/max</a><br /><br /><i>‘A fascinating and disturbing journey…Max is highly original and moving in its depiction of both the bravery and resilience of children, and their ability to engage in acts of evil. Author Sarah Cohen-Scali has written a unique, sensitive and morally complex depiction of two lost and damaged boys.’</i><br /><b>Readings</b><br /><br /><i>‘One of the most unique and impactful books that I’ve read for a long time. Even though Max is a child of evil, it’s tough not to love him and care for him..I challenge you all to not fall in love with Max and his story. Because it’s impossible.’</i><br /><b>Reading With Jenna</b><br /><br /><i>‘A fascinating fictionalisation of a very real piece of World War II history…Provocative and confronting.’</i><br /><b>Booktopia</b><br /><br /><i>‘Cohen-Scali’s research for the work is impeccable and her storytelling convincing. Max is a timely reminder of the tragedy of war.’</i><br /><b>Magpies</b><br /><br /><i>‘Compelling for young adult and adult readers alike…A must read.’</i><br /><b>New Zealand Book Council</b><br /><br /><i>‘A heartrending portrait of unlikely friendship and fierce defiance, and an impeccably researched glimpse into a deeply disturbing point in history. Unforgettable, bizarre, and brilliant.’</i><br /><b>Booklist</b><br /><br /><i>‘Readers will find Max’s story reminiscent of M.T. Anderson’s National Book Award-winning The Pox Party (2006). Horrific atrocities—and the ghastly realities of any war—seen through the eyes of a child with heartbreaking cognitive dissonance pack a wallop.’</i><br /><b>Kirkus Reviews</b></span>

<div id="review_1737561186" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1737561186" />
    <a title="Thehappymeerkat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47916020-thehappymeerkat"><img alt="Thehappymeerkat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444840805p2/47916020.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1737561186?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thehappymeerkat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thehappymeerkat" href="/user/show/47916020-thehappymeerkat">Thehappymeerkat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781406368253" href="/book/show/29326121-max">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47916020-thehappymeerkat?shelf=teen">teen</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47916020-thehappymeerkat?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47916020-thehappymeerkat?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1737561186" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer587600104246620688">I received this book for free from the publisher Walker Books in return for a review.<br/><br/>Max is the perfect Nazi baby. Born as part of the eugenics programme his blond hair, blue eyes and entire body are perfect. But raised with Nazi ideology and living through the brutality of war, how long can Max cope before reality questions his own beliefs?<br/><br/>'Max' is such an amazing and powerful novel it had me gripped, shocked, horrified and even had me crying! Told in the first person perspective throughout, Ma</span>
  <span id="freeText587600104246620688" style="display:none">I received this book for free from the publisher Walker Books in return for a review.<br /><br />Max is the perfect Nazi baby. Born as part of the eugenics programme his blond hair, blue eyes and entire body are perfect. But raised with Nazi ideology and living through the brutality of war, how long can Max cope before reality questions his own beliefs?<br /><br />'Max' is such an amazing and powerful novel it had me gripped, shocked, horrified and even had me crying! Told in the first person perspective throughout, Max tells us his story. It starts with him talking to us from inside the womb. This may seem odd but I got into the story straight away and I'm surprised how gripped I was from just the first few lines. As Max is born and begins to grow up he tells us his story though his own Nazi ideology. Although children may have not been born with that mindset, they were conditioned to think a certain way, and this is how Max thinks from the first moment we read. His attitude and opinions are full of hatred and are offensive, but it's the way the Nazis thought and this is what makes this novel so powerful.<br /><br />The book is separated into four parts. Each one a different time period in Max's life. As the book spans the timeframe of 1936 to 1945 Max doesn't grow up much in age but what he goes though and the experiences he has are far more mature than any nine year old should have gone through. The story is Max's and we learn about his life, growing up without love, conditioned to be the perfect future soldier, but as we read on other characters sometimes relay stories, or Max is able to see something happening which gives a greater insight into what was happening not only with the eugenics, titled the 'Lebensborn' programme, but we see other things that the Nazis did during the war. <br /><br />The horrors or war get more and more dark and shocking the further you read into the book. This may be a YA novel but it doesn't hold back from describing what really happened. You read about sickening and bloody deaths, sex, rape and many of the horrors including some of the details of the holocaust. This book is designed to shock and horrify and it really does. I've known a lot about the horrors of the Nazis in world war II both from what I learned at school and my various Polish extended family, but reading this stuff in a novel, when you feel you are part of the story was just all the more shocking and brutal. I liked the fact that towards the end, the story didn't hold back from telling the truth of what happened in the end of the war and didn't just focus on the brutality from the Nazis.<br /><br />Despite all the horrors I was so gripped by the novel that I only put it down when real life got in the way. The ending is a satisfying one but the whole book isn't a happy one. Apart from some quite graphic descriptions and mentions of deaths, there is use of the f and s swear words (as well as some milder ones) though this isn't too frequent. I usually don't enjoy too much swearing in novels but this felt so right and it made the story all the more intense.<br /><br />At the end of reading this novel I feel exhausted, I felt like crying too, but I'm glad I read this. Not only do you feel for this character Max as he's just a young child, and what he goes though isn't anything a child should go through, but at the same time you find yourself shocked at hearing the Nazi ideology come from him. It was really chilling to read at times but you still feel for this character and it really makes it all the harder to put this book down as you want to see what happens to him.<br /><br />I'd give this book a hundred stars if I could. It deserves them. It's so well written and you feel such emotions when reading it. It's a book that should be read by everyone, young and old, and it's the sort of book I wish I'd had when learning about the holocaust and war at school. It's endorsed by Amnesty International which isn't surprising. The authors notes at the end show how most of the characters are based on real people, making this again more chilling and dark. This book is sold as a YA novel but given the content I'd say older teens should read this and not anyone younger, the details are just too dark, but a book I'd recommend everyone around the world to pick up and read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="587600104246620688" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1932245066" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1932245066" />
    <a title="Drew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22104588-drew"><img alt="Drew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498412758p2/22104588.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1932245066?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Drew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Drew" href="/user/show/22104588-drew">Drew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781626720718" href="/book/show/25689006-max">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22104588-drew?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22104588-drew?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1932245066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13870050558118890775">3 1/2 stars. I can see why a lot of readers would hate this book. It's easy to despise the plot, to feel disgusted by the narrator, and the writing style takes a different turn than most historical fiction books. Yet this <b>appalling story was so fascinating to me.</b> I found the main character intriguing and couldn't seem to stop turning the pages.<br/><br/>This book follows Max, who literally starts his narration from the womb. Nearly ready to be born in 1936 in Nazi Germany, Max tells us in an "adult" voice</span>
  <span id="freeText13870050558118890775" style="display:none">3 1/2 stars. I can see why a lot of readers would hate this book. It's easy to despise the plot, to feel disgusted by the narrator, and the writing style takes a different turn than most historical fiction books. Yet this <b>appalling story was so fascinating to me.</b> I found the main character intriguing and couldn't seem to stop turning the pages.<br /><br />This book follows Max, who literally starts his narration from the womb. Nearly ready to be born in 1936 in Nazi Germany, Max tells us in an "adult" voice that he is part of the Lebensborn program and was carefully selected to be created from the Aryan race, which the Nazis consider to be the "purest" race.<br /><br />The Lebensborn program was an important part of history, where thousands of children were created to start a genetically "perfect" and "undefiled" race. Thousands of unmarried women believed it was their duty to have these children for Hitler, to be "bred" with Aryan or German strangers and give their babies up shortly after they were born to orphanages and German homes.<br /><br />When Max is born, he grows up in an orphanage under the Nazi Party and openly brags about the gruesome details of his life. He is quickly taken away from his mother after he is born, and Max tells us that it's hard to let her go, but he must be tough for the Führer. At an incredibly young age, Max witnesses the cruelty of the Nazis - and, of course, he is brainwashed into thinking they are the good guys - including racism and physical violence against the Jews.<br /><br />I feel like I have to issue a warning that this book is <b>incredibly graphic.</b> Max doesn't shy away from giving detailed descriptions about prostitutes he sees with German soldiers at parties, the horrible treatment of Jews (the "inspection" scene of Jewish girls sent chills down my spine), or the rough treatment of young children. The shock of Max using words like "whore" to describe women - and much worse - quickly wore off, or else I wouldn't have been able to read on.<br /><br />When Max befriends an older boy, Lukas, the two quickly become close, and Max admires Lukas's bravado and rebellious spirit. But when Max discovers Lukas is a Jew hiding in plain sight, pretending to be a Polish boy, Max is torn - he isn't sure whether he should turn Lukas in to the Nazi soldiers he's trusted his whole life, or if he should stay loyal to his friend and help Lukas escape.<br /><br />I hugely appreciated the gritty tone Max used to tell this story - he didn't sugarcoat anything that was happening around him but told it like it was - <b>a horrible time in history.</b> The fact that such young children really <i>were</i> in this awful environment in Nazi Germany hit me hard. I can't imagine how hard this book was to write, and the author told it in the most <b>revolting, sickening manner possible,</b> while still making it a fast-paced and intriguing read. And I'm grateful to her for that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13870050558118890775" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1573169003" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1573169003" />
    <a title="Jacki (Julia Flyte)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2095931-jacki-julia-flyte"><img alt="Jacki (Julia Flyte)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494360994p2/2095931.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1573169003?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacki (Julia Flyte)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacki (Julia Flyte)" href="/user/show/2095931-jacki-julia-flyte">Jacki (Julia Flyte)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781922182852" href="/book/show/28231016-max">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1573169003" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16121817504169110842">This is the story of a child born under the Nazi "Lebensborn" program, which was essentially a program to breed pure Ayran babies who would be the future leaders of the Third Reich. Max is born on April 20, 1936, making him the first baby to roll off the production line. He is also the narrator of this story which covers not just the Lebensborn program but also the abduction and "germanification" of suitably Ayran children from other countries and the lives of ordinary Germans as the war drew to</span>
  <span id="freeText16121817504169110842" style="display:none">This is the story of a child born under the Nazi "Lebensborn" program, which was essentially a program to breed pure Ayran babies who would be the future leaders of the Third Reich. Max is born on April 20, 1936, making him the first baby to roll off the production line. He is also the narrator of this story which covers not just the Lebensborn program but also the abduction and "germanification" of suitably Ayran children from other countries and the lives of ordinary Germans as the war drew to a close. Real people and events are woven into the story.<br /><br />Max is a lively narrator who is initially very euphoric about all things Nazi and who can't wait to grow up and fight for the Fatherland. While he never knows his mother, he understands that she was selected and rigorously examined to ensure that she was a perfect Ayran specimen. He is indoctrinated in all things Nazi from infancy and therefore is very matter of fact about the abduction of Polish children - in his eyes they are lucky to be receiving a German upbringing. However things become murkier when he befriends Lukas, a blonde and blue eyed Pole who is secretly Jewish. Gradually his enthusiasm will wane and his disenchantment will grow over the course of the war.<br /><br />My central issue with the story is Max's youth. He has only just turned nine when the war ends. Essentially, his narration is a device and you have to suspend disbelief that a child would think the things that he does and just go with the story - which he starts telling us when he's still a foetus in the womb.<br /><br />Originally published in France in 2012, Max has only recently been translated into English. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16121817504169110842" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1088857121" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1088857121" />
    <a title="Mélina.g" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28261509-m-lina-g"><img alt="Mélina.g" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1088857121?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mélina.g" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mélina.g" href="/user/show/28261509-m-lina-g">Mélina.g</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1088857121" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3541922420523792130">Let me tell you one thing about this book. I won't consider it as a masterpiece, I won't call it a life changing novel (that's why I only gave 3 stars by the way) but I would say without any hesitation that "Max" is a book that will make you think. A lot. <br/>First of all, it's the story of Max, actually Konrad, who is a boy born to represent the aryen race at is best, during WWII. Basically, Hitler wanted to create an army of nazis pupets. So, our main character is one of them. We follow his story </span>
  <span id="freeText3541922420523792130" style="display:none">Let me tell you one thing about this book. I won't consider it as a masterpiece, I won't call it a life changing novel (that's why I only gave 3 stars by the way) but I would say without any hesitation that "Max" is a book that will make you think. A lot. <br />First of all, it's the story of Max, actually Konrad, who is a boy born to represent the aryen race at is best, during WWII. Basically, Hitler wanted to create an army of nazis pupets. So, our main character is one of them. We follow his story from his birth to the end of WWII. <br />I found it a little bit too long to start, and I actually started to enjoy it more with the arrival of a new character: Lukas. The main character being made to be the perfect little nazi, isn't one you can relate to easily. He isn't one of those character that you love from the first moment. But eventually, considering the fact that you know all of his life since his birth, you're able to understand why he act like he does, why he thinks what he thinks...<br />It's also rough as a story, obviously it's about WWII, but some scene can be quite explicite and the language can be crude. It's not a story for young children. <br />Beside that, the story itself is one of those that really leave you thinking. I actually finished the second half of the book in one sitting, at 1:30 am. Because you find yourself carring about the characters, you find yourself wanting an happy ending for them. Cause they're not evil. They're just products of the Nazi's government. They're victims of it. And even though I had some problems with this book, I finished it thinking differently about WWII. You can only admire the research and the effort of the author, who reveal the nazi regim's horror, way more that what you learn in school can teach you, in a very deeper way. You end up being able to see how far they were ready to go, and what the world could have been if the nazis hadn't been defeated. <br />I will end up on this note: if you love history, you should read this book. But expect it to be different from what you think it is, and expect to learn a lot from it. Actually, even if you don't love history, you should read it. Because I think everyone should be aware of what happened. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3541922420523792130" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1736499180" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1736499180" />
    <a title="Valentina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49763566-valentina"><img alt="Valentina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495820133p2/49763566.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1736499180?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Valentina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Valentina" href="/user/show/49763566-valentina">Valentina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1736499180" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15495908880381613171">The second world war and nazism are probably the topics there are more books about, but I am always surprised by the originality of some. <br/><br/>I would classify Max as a coming of age story, but obviously it is very different from other books belonging to this category that you might have read, being essentially the coming of age story of a little nazi. <br/><br/>We follow Max's life from when he is still in his mother's belly to around the age of ten. He is the first baby produced by the Lebensborn program, in</span>
  <span id="freeText15495908880381613171" style="display:none">The second world war and nazism are probably the topics there are more books about, but I am always surprised by the originality of some. <br /><br />I would classify Max as a coming of age story, but obviously it is very different from other books belonging to this category that you might have read, being essentially the coming of age story of a little nazi. <br /><br />We follow Max's life from when he is still in his mother's belly to around the age of ten. He is the first baby produced by the Lebensborn program, initially ideated by Henrich Himmler whose aim was to pare people of pure aryan race to make them produce a pure aryan baby that they would donate to Hitler to replace the judish population. <br /><br />This is a first person narration, it is always Max who speaks, even when he is just a fetus. Max has really evil thoughs, he does not think and act like a normal child at all, Max is a nazi even before being born. Obviously this is the hyperbole that the author decides to use to show that an ideology as rooted as nazism prescind individuality and age, sometimes there is no escape, especially if that is what you have always been taught. <br /><br />In my opinion this novel is as harsh as brilliant. It breakes the mith that children are always innocent and good and at the same time highlights how important the atmosphere you grow in is. I really liked it, I recommend it to everyone, because this book is really brave and it carries really interesting concepts in a really interesting way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15495908880381613171" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3429255337" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3429255337" />
    <a title="Valentine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68334549-valentine"><img alt="Valentine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603573724p2/68334549.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3429255337?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Valentine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Valentine" href="/user/show/68334549-valentine">Valentine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68334549-valentine?shelf=standalone">standalone</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68334549-valentine?shelf=2-stars">2-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68334549-valentine?shelf=read-in-french">read-in-french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68334549-valentine?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3429255337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4103444738065708922">What a painful, painful read. This actually wasn't my first time with this book: I had tried to read it several years ago, but I couldn't even finish it because of the writing style. Flash-forward to the present, I was watching <i>The Man In The High Castle</i> and I got very interested in the story of the Lebensborn - which reminded me of this book that dealt with the same topic. I then thought that, being way older, I would perhaps have a different perspective on it and perhaps even enjoy it.<br/><br/>Spoiler </span>
  <span id="freeText4103444738065708922" style="display:none">What a painful, painful read. This actually wasn't my first time with this book: I had tried to read it several years ago, but I couldn't even finish it because of the writing style. Flash-forward to the present, I was watching <i>The Man In The High Castle</i> and I got very interested in the story of the Lebensborn - which reminded me of this book that dealt with the same topic. I then thought that, being way older, I would perhaps have a different perspective on it and perhaps even enjoy it.<br /><br />Spoiler alert: I didn't.<br /><br />Thing is, I understand the value of this book as a reminder of how awful that part of history was. I was actually interested in seeing how the Lebensborn project was created, but also how the main character would evolve through such an unstable world - and eventually the defeat of the Reich he used to value so much. But thing is, I still understood that the first time I read it, back when I was fifteen or so. At the time, I felt a little uncomfortable giving a negative opinion about this book, considering the heavy, crucial topics it dealt with, but now I'm not afraid to say it this way: <b>it's not because a book is "serious" that it's good.</b><br /><br />Because that's what was my biggest issue with it in the end. As a person, I acknowledge its purpose in teaching people about that dark period; but as a reader, my opinion is much different. I'm going to repeat myself, but the only reason I gave it 2 stars and not 1 is solely because there's much to learn about history in it, and that it seems that the author actually did a lot of serious research to write her story. That's it. <br /><br />Otherwise, I have no shame in saying I hated this book. My main problem was with the writing: it's a question of personal preferences, so I'm just stating what <i>I</i> felt reading it. <b>It's gross, crude, vulgar, and utterly disgusting.</b> I usually have no problem with swearing or rude words, I literally don't care, but there it was just atrocious. I don't know if reading it in its original French version made it worse, but I was literally cringing my way through it. There's basically not a single sentence without a swear word or a graphic description. Yeah, sure, you could argue that it was in order to represent the difficulty of the times and also a device to show how the Reich managed to turn an innocent child into a literal little monster with no feelings. Sure. I think it could have worked if it had been used cleverly, but just not this way. It's not because you make a four-year old say horrible things, swear, call all women whores and describe sexual scenes extremely explicitly that it makes him a complex, fleshed-out character. It's just lazy writing, being shocking just for the sake of being shocking. It was so exaggerated and so badly used that it just became annoying and unbelievable. Same goes with the characterization: I have no problem with a small child following Nazi ideologies, that's literally what brainwashing and the desired upbringing of the regime was about. What bothered me most was how un-childlike the narrator sounded - and not in a "this poor kid had to become a grown-up to survive" kind of way. No, rather in a "I've never met a four year-old in my life but I'm pretty sure they can articulate extremely developped thoughts and sentences, just like an adult." Sure thing.<br /><br />I can't say this was a disappointing read because I didn't expect anything from it. As I said, I had already disliked the writing years ago, and I still <i>hated</i> it today. I'm honestly kind of sick of people thinking they <i>have</i> to say good things about books with such sensitive topics. You can find historical value in it while criticizing the way the author has constructed their story. It's what having a critical mind is all about. I'm not pretending to understand every single layer inside the book, and I'm definitely not saying I know what the author was thinking when she wrote it - I'm just trying to explain why I despised this book so much. <br /><br />To me, this book just didn't bring anything new to the table. If you know nothing about World War II or the Holocaust, then yes it might be useful to you - but honestly I wouldn't recommend it for this either. If you're a little more familiar with the subject, you may not learn anything new, or at least not in a way that's interesting and original. The story is poorly constructed, with useless, crude moments being described for paragraphs while important ones are skipped over. The writing is extremely simplistic, and honestly it sometimes sounded like a child or a young teen wrote it. It's crass and vulgar, and not even in a deep, shocking way. It's just <i>there</i>. It's just sad that the style was so awful it made hate a book that could have been life-changing, that's all I'm saying.<br /><br />Long story short, what I'm saying is that you don't need to go all edgy on your book to make your point. Writing something that is not overly vulgar for no reason does not make it less shocking or eye-opening. There are plenty of books out there that are written in a very poetic way, or just nicely, and that will hit you ten times harder than that one. I'm also thinking of the movie <i>Jojo Rabbit</i>, which is excellent in my opinion: it's literally about a young Nazi boy realizing he's been taught horrible things all his life. The movie made me wail and bawl my eyes out, and was extremely vivid about the horrors of the Reich and the war. But Hell! It was literally a dark comedy and it wasn't vulgar <i>at all.</i> It's just personal preferences of course, but that just proves <b>you don't have to be graphic to be shocking.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="4103444738065708922" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1524509238" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1524509238" />
    <a title="Živa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11280015-iva"><img alt="Živa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536403400p2/11280015.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1524509238?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Živa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Živa" href="/user/show/11280015-iva">Živa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781922182852" href="/book/show/28231016-max">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1524509238" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16910217530719558589">So, first of all, I've just read the book. I think it is awesome reading. If you loved Diary of the young girl - Anne Frank or Wir Kinder vom Bahnhof Zoo, you'd love this too. It is not such a personal book, but the author did an excellent research and wrote an inspirating, pleasant and educational story. That is where spoilers begin ... just a warning.<br/><br/><a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[At the beginning I had a hard time with starting read because of its style of writing. It is spoken in the first person in point o</span></span>
  <span id="freeText16910217530719558589" style="display:none">So, first of all, I've just read the book. I think it is awesome reading. If you loved Diary of the young girl - Anne Frank or Wir Kinder vom Bahnhof Zoo, you'd love this too. It is not such a personal book, but the author did an excellent research and wrote an inspirating, pleasant and educational story. That is where spoilers begin ... just a warning.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[At the beginning I had a hard time with starting read because of its style of writing. It is spoken in the first person in point of view from Max, which tells his story from beginning indeed. He is still in the womb, and we discover he is a part of the Lebensborn, for what we know it is a Nazi program to make German Aryan children - pure breed blond and blue eyed children. We learn that they separate Max from his birth mother, and then he grows in the nursery. He shares his birthday with Adolf Hitler thus the Nazi people treat Max specially. In Lebensborn they calculate everything, everything is noted, everything is stringent laws. One day Max was kidnapped by wasted Jewess prisoner and almost died from dehydration. But he survived so he is a real Aryan boy. Max then moves in Poland, where he with Bibiana works under the veil of they are native Poland and their task is discovering blue eyed and colored Polish children. The SS soldiers break in homes and separate kids from their mothers. It is a move calculated to "change" Polish children into Aryan children and to be adopted by German families. Then we discover they relocate Max in an army school, where is Nazi propaganda at full. He becomes aware of one boy, which is renamed as Lukas though he is reluctant and repeats he is Lucjan (his Polish name) and insult doctor Ebner, who lead the selection of boys and girls. Max or now called Konrad saves this boy life. Lukas is, at first, angry and does not want to have a business with Konrad, but then, when Konrad jumped by Lukas's side to save him from hard whipping, Lukas tells Konrad his life story. He is a Polish Jew, he was not circumcised (because his mother didn't want it) and so he easily passed as blue eyed and blond haired Pole. He saw his family went into a ghetto and first his little brother died, then father. About his mother destiny, he does not know anything. Lukas reveals Konrad he killed two of his "colleagues" as revenge. They send Lukas to army training which is a brute. Lukas likes to contradict and object to Konrad's affirmations, and he know a lot about what is happening with European Jews. We discover that the soap with it the boys use in a bath is made from human - (no word for this). Hitler's Germany is decayed very rapidly and the real chaos start. Max and Lukas escape to Berlin, where they hide in the underground. People know Ivans are coming (Russian army) and that allies destroyed Hitler's army. The women are scared because they know what will happen. As Konrad watched German soldiers "mating" with whores or (raping) Polish women, he knows what is going on with his female neighbors in the basement. Russian rape women regularly and they prefer obese women, but they do spare children because they said the German did the same to their wives and murdered children. Lukas and a girl named Ute become a couple and they hide in the apartment of one of Max's classmate, Manfred. For some time it seems it will end well. But no, one day a drunken Russian soldier follows Manfred when he looked for food, and he kills Lukas because he finds a photo. A photo of the blonde woman pictured with Hitler and her young baby, we discover he was Max's mother and that she was his neighbour in the basement until Russian killed her because she couldn't destroy the photo. Max decides he would go to the south, to find Americans as he and Lucas saw in a dream. He is almost dead and undernourished when English nurses find him. Max tells his story. So the book ends. I must say I enjoyed this book, but its themes and events sit hard on my soul because I became accustomed to Lukas and other unfortunate victims. I hated Max at first but now I understand he was indoctrinated and educated in Nazi tenet, but he changed his opinion and viewpoint after meeting with Lukas. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />It is a fiction book, but with well-researched details and historically accurate. It should be on reading badge for schools and adults should read this book too. I recommend this.</span>

<div id="review_1863445478" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1863445478?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica Gillies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica Gillies" href="/user/show/32087928-jessica-gillies">Jessica Gillies</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781406368253" href="/book/show/29326121-max">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32087928-jessica-gillies?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32087928-jessica-gillies?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32087928-jessica-gillies?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1863445478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15916046547307920651">"...I really miss it. Since I've been born, I've done nothing but obey orders, and now there's no one to tell me what to do."<br/><br/>What a difficult book to rate. This is the sort of book that is difficult to put down, but when you do , it leaves you feeling emotionally exhausted.<br/><br/>The author has used a very unique voice in her story, not only that of a child in the midst of war, and not only a Nazi child, but one which is totally immersed in the regime even from before their birth, and is commited to it</span>
  <span id="freeText15916046547307920651" style="display:none">"...I really miss it. Since I've been born, I've done nothing but obey orders, and now there's no one to tell me what to do."<br /><br />What a difficult book to rate. This is the sort of book that is difficult to put down, but when you do , it leaves you feeling emotionally exhausted.<br /><br />The author has used a very unique voice in her story, not only that of a child in the midst of war, and not only a Nazi child, but one which is totally immersed in the regime even from before their birth, and is commited to it. The novel serves not only to show the effects of Max/Konrad's indoctrination, but also sheds light on parts of WWII and the Nazi regime I didn't know much about before , such as the Lebensborn programme and the kidnapping and Germanisation of Polish children.<br /><br />While this story has been classified as young adult, I would say it's for a more mature young adult. As the cover says , it"dares to speak of the unspeakable". It is not excessively gruesome, but doesn't romanticise or cover up the reality of events in any way.<br /><br />I don't know if I could handle re-reading this, but I learnt so much from it, that if the author publishes more books, I will certainly be interested.</span>

<div id="review_2195009257" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2195009257?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bogi Takács" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bogi Takács" href="/user/show/29924068-bogi-tak-cs">Bogi Takács</a>

          added it

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781626720718" href="/book/show/25689006-max">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2195009257" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4026408409635803401">Review coming. Actually a solid book, creepy and awful, but creepy and awful in the same ways as the historical setting it describes. There is a fine balance in how explicit to make such descriptions (Octavia Butler talked a lot about this related to her novel Kindred, about African-American slavery) and I think especially toward the end, Max wobbled in this respect, but I still got through it very fast. It might be too explicit for an American YA audience, I have to think about that. It's not p</span>
  <span id="freeText4026408409635803401" style="display:none">Review coming. Actually a solid book, creepy and awful, but creepy and awful in the same ways as the historical setting it describes. There is a fine balance in how explicit to make such descriptions (Octavia Butler talked a lot about this related to her novel Kindred, about African-American slavery) and I think especially toward the end, Max wobbled in this respect, but I still got through it very fast. It might be too explicit for an American YA audience, I have to think about that. It's not perfect, and I do have some issues with it - as I'm planning on discussing in detail, G-d willing -, but it's one of the stronger YA books I read this year.<br /><br />I have to say that now I am even more upset that so many American Jewish authors trashed this book unread. Racism against Mizrachi Jews + Jews of color in general (the author is a Moroccan Jewish woman from France) is a huge intra-community issue and should NOT be perpetuated. Discussing the book as if it wasn't even written by a Jewish author is outright vile. Sure, non-Jews might not know that "Cohen" is a Jewish name, but to play into that... :(((<br /><br />Detailed warnings also coming, but this is a book just as brutal as its subject matter and has depictions of violence, sexual assault, murder and also descriptions of post-traumatic stress.<br /><br />Source of the book: Lawrence Public Library</span>

<div id="review_1644477216" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1644477216" />
    <a title="Rabiah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3177566-rabiah"><img alt="Rabiah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502856157p2/3177566.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1644477216?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rabiah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rabiah" href="/user/show/3177566-rabiah">Rabiah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781406368253" href="/book/show/29326121-max">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3177566-rabiah?shelf=arc">arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3177566-rabiah?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1644477216" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12676348157841479410">Originally posted at: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://iliveforreading.blogspot.com/2016/08/max-by-sarah-cohen-scali.html'>https://iliveforreading.blogspot.com/...</a><br/><br/>I had to steel myself before I started reading Max. I love young adult fiction that concerns history, but with an Amnesty International endorsement, this one was bound to be heavy. What. A. Book. This was probably THE most powerful book I've read this year. While I don't cry when it comes to books, this one had me stunned and shocked the whole way through.<br/><br/>World War II isn't an easy subject to read about, but I've read several young ad</span>
  <span id="freeText12676348157841479410" style="display:none">Originally posted at: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://iliveforreading.blogspot.com/2016/08/max-by-sarah-cohen-scali.html">https://iliveforreading.blogspot.com/...</a><br /><br />I had to steel myself before I started reading Max. I love young adult fiction that concerns history, but with an Amnesty International endorsement, this one was bound to be heavy. What. A. Book. This was probably THE most powerful book I've read this year. While I don't cry when it comes to books, this one had me stunned and shocked the whole way through.<br /><br />World War II isn't an easy subject to read about, but I've read several young adult novels set in during this period of time, and I've studied the war in several history and social science classes. However, this is the first time I've ever heard about the Lebensborn program. For those who don't know, the Lebensborn program was a secret project to raise the birth rate and "purify" the Aryan race in Germany during the second World War. It promoted Nazi eugenics, and also included the Germanisation of Polish children. Cohen-Scali integrated this so well into the story: I learned so much about the project through the eyes of Konrad, and it's horrifying to know that this actually happened to children, and there were actually people capable of doing this to others.<br /><br />Konrad was such a unique protagonist. We learn about the world from the moment he's in the womb. Although he's a lot younger than many protagonists in young adult fiction, this child is put through so much right from a young age. This book deals with sexual assault, death, anti-Semitism, and so much more, and we as readers are thrown into the world of a boy engineered to follow Hitler's rule. Reading it from his point of view is probably the toughest thing ever, because you see how he's essentially brain-washed from birth into following the awful propaganda that Hitler and the Nazi party preached. However, you also do see the moments of doubt, where Konrad figures that certain things don't add up, or the moments where you see the child and not the machine.<br /><br />Utterly heartbreaking and mind numbingly brilliant, Max is a novel I urge you to read. Scali's beautiful prose captures the horrific reality of the second World War through the eyes of a child controlled by the third Reich as he struggles to identify the truth between two clashing ideologies. Beautiful, frightening and arresting, this remarkable novel is one that cannot be missed.<br /><br />▪ ▪ ▪ Thank you so much to Sasha from Pansing for sending me a copy for review! ▪ ▪ ▪</span>
  <a data-text-id="12676348157841479410" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1789693882" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1789693882" />
    <a title="Talley Gaskins" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13691163-talley-gaskins"><img alt="Talley Gaskins" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1350426681p2/13691163.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1789693882?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Talley Gaskins" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Talley Gaskins" href="/user/show/13691163-talley-gaskins">Talley Gaskins</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1789693882" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5213122228103926029">I understand that this book was translated from it's original language and maybe some of the brilliance got lost in that translation. For me I wanted to like this book, I wanted to appreciate that the author was trying something new with the perspective at the start of the story. But I tried and failed to like this book. I am usually the first to advocate for suspension of disbelief but it doesn't work here. There are just TOO many things that are IMPOSSIBLE to me. It just doesn't work. HOW is t</span>
  <span id="freeText5213122228103926029" style="display:none">I understand that this book was translated from it's original language and maybe some of the brilliance got lost in that translation. For me I wanted to like this book, I wanted to appreciate that the author was trying something new with the perspective at the start of the story. But I tried and failed to like this book. I am usually the first to advocate for suspension of disbelief but it doesn't work here. There are just TOO many things that are IMPOSSIBLE to me. It just doesn't work. HOW is this fetus able to give us SO VERY MUCH historical background about this program that conceived him, his mother's thoughts and feelings that he seems so in tune with one second, but completely baffle him the next. The complete pride and devotion that a fetus feels toward Hitler and the regime when it isn't clear that that is how his mother feels...so where does all that awareness and conviction come from? How. HOW!!!! Is the biggest question I was asking myself the entire time. I just couldn't buy it and it ruined everything for me. Which is sad because there is a lot of historically relevant and interesting information to be gleaned here, if you can make it past the really terrible and unconvincing point of view. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5213122228103926029" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3133166779" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3133166779" />
    <a title="Rachel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/107756378-rachel"><img alt="Rachel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586450179p2/107756378.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3133166779?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel" href="/user/show/107756378-rachel">Rachel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3133166779" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9095136902464647195">Overall, this is a detailed and researched insight into the lebensborn programme, a Nazi enterprise that sought to breed ‘racially pure’ children to be the leaders of the third reich. The story is narrated my the first child of this programme, from before his birth until he is 10 and the Second World War has ended. I found it exhausting but important. It was not unbearably gruesome but, despite the narrator seeing and supporting the horrific events of the SS (in particular), it did not at all gl</span>
  <span id="freeText9095136902464647195" style="display:none">Overall, this is a detailed and researched insight into the lebensborn programme, a Nazi enterprise that sought to breed ‘racially pure’ children to be the leaders of the third reich. The story is narrated my the first child of this programme, from before his birth until he is 10 and the Second World War has ended. I found it exhausting but important. It was not unbearably gruesome but, despite the narrator seeing and supporting the horrific events of the SS (in particular), it did not at all glorify or romanticise them. <br />The language used was always going to be strange at times- the narrative voice of the book begins as an unborn foetus yet speaks eloquently in full sentences. Moreover some of the language seems a little off at times given the context, however this could be due to the translation. <br />It is true, as many other seems to find, that Max never obviously expresses any great revelation that he has been raised wrongly, and he maintains his arrogant and conceited tone almost entirely throughout. But I liked this- he expresses remorse through actions rather than words. I also found it to be more realistic of the difficulty that comes with unlearning an entire belief system that you’ve been taught to idolise. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9095136902464647195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1482792446" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1482792446" />
    <a title="Zoe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3541974-zoe"><img alt="Zoe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1270474177p2/3541974.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1482792446?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zoe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zoe" href="/user/show/3541974-zoe">Zoe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1482792446" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12294140414898722907">Perhaps the most distressing novel I've ever read, this visceral, brutal and shocking account of one aspect of of Nazi racial ideology, the Lebensborn programme, movingingly shows how complex and desperate war is. It's the sort of book that leaves you undone and changed, and realising that you may have held your breath (or at least felt like you were doing so) for the entire heart-pummelling novel. A brilliantly woven narrative draws ever tighter, asking huge questions about what motivates evil </span>
  <span id="freeText12294140414898722907" style="display:none">Perhaps the most distressing novel I've ever read, this visceral, brutal and shocking account of one aspect of of Nazi racial ideology, the Lebensborn programme, movingingly shows how complex and desperate war is. It's the sort of book that leaves you undone and changed, and realising that you may have held your breath (or at least felt like you were doing so) for the entire heart-pummelling novel. A brilliantly woven narrative draws ever tighter, asking huge questions about what motivates evil acts and about how we construct our identities, about about bearing witness and the grey areas around victims and perpetrators. <br /><br />Told in memoir format by a child born into the Lebensborn programme the language is explicit and harsh from the outset. Normally I cannot bring myself to face such brutality, but Cohen-Scali has written a page turner, gripping and audacious, as hard to put down as it is to stomach. As with Elizabeth Wein's Rose Under Fire, finishing Max felt like an important act of self-education and of acknowledging and illuminating some truly terrible aspects of humanity. <br /><br />Breathtaking, heartbreaking, devastating. And equally astonishingly brilliant.<br /><br />NB The edition of Max which I actually read is the English translation by Penny Hueston, due to be published by Walker Books in 2016 ISBN 978 1 4063 6825-3, not listed in Goodreads at the time of writing my thoughts about the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12294140414898722907" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
